Actions That Violate Muru'ah and Their Effect on Testimony
Ruling on testimony of those with lacking muru'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify actions that violate muru'ah and state the condition under which they disqualify a witness according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Even permissible actions can disqualify a witness if they show habitual lack of modesty.

The Hanafi school requires witnesses to possess moral probity (muru'ah). This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists specific behaviors that indicate a lack of modesty, such as urinating or eating in public. However, the key condition is persistence—occasional lapses may not affect credibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage deals with witness testimony and actions violating muru'ah. The overall ruling is that such actions, if habitual, disqualify a witness.
2
List the examples given
The passage lists: urinating/eating on the road, stretching legs in public, wrestling of youths in the mosque, stealing a morsel, excessive joking, keeping company with lowlifes, and clowning.
These are illustrative; any action violating muru'ah is included.
3
Apply the principle of 'idman'
The text states that persistence (idman) should be required. An isolated act does not necessarily indicate a defective character.
This is a crucial nuance: occasional indiscretions do not disqualify.
4
Distinguish from other fiqh schools
Some schools may not require muru'ah to the same degree, or may not require persistence. Hanafi position is stricter in protecting courtroom integrity.
The ikhtilaf is in the weight given to these actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يبول أو يأكل على الطريق لأنه يدل على قلة الحياء. (عيني) وكذا كل فعل يخل بالمروءة، وإن لم يكن حراماً كمد رجل عند الناس ومصارعة الأحداث في المسجد، وسرقة لقمة، والإفراط في المزاح المفضي إلى الاستخفاف، وصحبة الأراذل والتمسخر، وينبغي اشتراط الإدمان في هذه الأشياء. (فتح)
Or he urinates or eats on the road, because it indicates lack of modesty. (al-'Ayni) And likewise any action that violates muru'ah, even if not haram, such as stretching one's leg in front of people, wrestling of youths in the mosque, stealing a morsel, excessive joking that leads to belittlement, keeping company with lowlifes, and clowning. It is appropriate to stipulate persistence in these things. (al-Fath)
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage lists actions that indicate lack of moral probity (muru'ah) and thus disqualify a witness. They include urinating or eating on the road because they show shamelessness. Examples extend to any behavior lacking dignity: stretching legs in public, wrestling in mosque, petty theft, excessive joking, keeping bad company, and clowning. The author notes that persistence ('idman) should be required for such actions to affect testimony—occasional lapses may not disqualify.

Key Terms
المروءة (al-muru'ah): Moral probity or decency; adherence to social norms of good character.
قلة الحياء (qillat al-haya'): Lack of modesty or shame, considered a defect in character.
الإدمان (al-idman): Persistence or habituation in an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for actions that violate muru'ah to disqualify a witness according to the passage?
A) The action must be haram.
B) The action must be done once.
C) The action must be habitual (idman).
D) The action must be done secretly.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a witness may be disqualified not only by sin but also by any habitual action that indicates lack of modesty.

Ruling on Testimony of One Who Curses the Salaf
Ruling on testimony of one who curses the Salaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a witness who curses the Salaf, distinguish between open and concealed cursing, and apply Abu Yusuf's distinction between cursing and disavowal.

Core Idea
Open cursing of the Salaf disqualifies a witness; concealment does not; cursing any Muslim indicates lack of muru'ah.

The testimony of a person of upright character ('adalah) is essential in Islamic courts. This lesson examines a specific case: a witness who curses the righteous predecessors (al-salaf). The text distinguishes between open display and concealment, and introduces a nuanced view from Abu Yusuf regarding disavowal versus cursing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon: the act of openly cursing the Salaf. The ruling: this act constitutes fisq (moral depravity), which disqualifies the person from giving testimony.
The ruling applies only when the cursing is open (zuhur), not concealed.
2
Locate the key condition
The text restricts the ruling to open cursing (yuzhir). If the person conceals his cursing, his testimony is accepted. Understand why: concealment means the person does not publicly display defiance, so his 'adalah may still be intact.
This is a conditional ruling tied to the concept of 'adalah and public integrity.
3
Trace the reasoning
Cursing indicates lack of muru'ah (sense of honor) and deficient intellect, which impairs 'adalah. The text suggests this applies to cursing any Muslim, not just the Salaf, but the Salaf are explicitly mentioned for emphasis.
The reasoning is based on general legal principles, not a specific textual proof.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
Note Abu Yusuf's position: he distinguishes between cursing (sabb) and disavowal (tabarra'). He accepts testimony from one who disavows the Companions because they consider it their religion, not fisq. Understand the basis of his distinction.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority position within the Hanafi school; the majority holds that disavowal also shows lack of respect and may affect testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يظهر سب السلف [يعني الصالحين وهم الصحابة والتابعون والعلماء كأبي حنيفة وأصحابه. (عيني)] قيد بالظهور؛ لأنه لو كتمه تقبل شهادته، ولو قال: "أو يظهر سب مسلم" لكان أولى؛ لأن العدالة تسقط مسلم، وإن لم يكن من السلف؛ لأن السب يدل على قلة المروءة وقصور العقل، وعن أبي يوسف: لا أقبل شهادة من سب الصحابة، وأقبلها ممن تبرأ منهم؛ لأنهم يعتقدون ديناً، وإن كان على باطل، فلم يظهر فسقه، بخلاف الساب. (عيني، فتح)
Or he openly curses the salaf [meaning the righteous, who are the Companions, Followers, and scholars like Abu Hanifa and his companions. (al-'Ayni)] It is restricted to open display; because if he conceals it, his testimony is accepted. And if he said 'or he openly curses a Muslim' it would be more appropriate; because uprightness is nullified for (cursing) a Muslim, even if not from the salaf; because cursing indicates lack of muru'ah and deficiency of intellect. And from Abu Yusuf: I do not accept the testimony of one who curses the Companions, but I accept it from one who disavows them; because they consider it a religion, even if it is false, so his fisq is not apparent, unlike the curser. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath)
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage deals with a witness who openly curses the salaf (righteous predecessors including Companions, Followers, and scholars). The key distinction is between open cursing (zuhur) and concealment: if done openly, the witness is considered fasiq and testimony rejected; if concealed, testimony is accepted. The author notes that it would be better to say 'curses a Muslim' because any Muslim's cursing indicates lack of muru'ah. Ibn Abi Layla's position is mentioned: Abu Yusuf does not accept testimony of one who curses Companions but accepts from those who disavow them, because the latter follow a misguided belief but not fisq.

Key Terms
السلف (al-salaf): Righteous predecessors: Companions, Followers, and leading scholars like Abu Hanifa.
السب (al-sabb): Cursing or reviling, considered a major sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, whose testimony is accepted among those who have incorrect beliefs about the Companions?
A) One who curses them.
B) One who disavows them.
C) Both.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
Open cursing of the Salaf disqualifies a witness; concealment does not; Abu Yusuf accepts testimony from one who disavows them.

Testimony for Relatives: Hanafi Acceptance with Conditions
Ruling on testimony of relatives and in-laws within Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the relatives for whom testimony is accepted in Hanafi fiqh and state the condition that must be met.

Core Idea
Testimony for relatives is accepted unless litigation exists—suspicion only arises when conflict is active.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all relatives' testimonies are rejected due to suspicion. This lesson identifies specific relatives (brother, uncle, in-laws by rada', etc.) for whom testimony is accepted because their interests are distinct. A key condition—absence of litigation—prevents bias. The passage also notes a consensus claim and contrasts with the Maliki school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed relatives
Read the passage and list all relatives for whom testimony is accepted. These include brother, uncle, parents by rada', and certain in-laws.
The list is explicit and exhaustive for this rule.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason for acceptance is the absence of tuhmah (suspicion), because these relatives have separate properties and interests.
Tuhmah is the core concept in Hanafi shahadat law.
3
Note the critical condition
The condition: there must be no ongoing litigation between the witness and the relative. If litigation exists, the testimony is rejected.
The condition is from the commentary by al-'Ayni and al-Fath.
4
Examine the consensus claim
The text claims this ruling is by consensus (ijma'), but note that the Maliki school disagrees, so the consensus may not be universal.
Understanding the scope of ijma' is important.
5
Compare with the Maliki position
Malik rejects testimony if the witness lives in the same household or if the relative receives substantial benefit. This is a stricter standard.
The ikhtilaf is due to different assessments of tuhmah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقبل لأخيه وعمّه وأبويه رضاعاً وأمّ امرأته وبنتها وزوج بنته وامرأة ابنه وأبيه وأهل الهوى أي ولأجل امرأة ابنه لعدم التهمة وهذا بالإجماع. واعلم أن قبول الشهادة للأخ والعم والمحرم رضاعاً أو مصاهرة مقيد بما إذا لم يخاصم معه. (عيني، فتح) وعند مالك: لو كانا في عياله لا تقبل، وعنه: لو يصل إليهما نفعا كثيراً لا تقبل.
And testimony is accepted for his brother, his uncle, his parents by rada', his wife's mother and daughter, his daughter's husband, his son's wife, his father, and the people of hawa—that is, for the sake of his son's wife, due to absence of tuhmah, and this is by consensus. Know that acceptance of testimony for brother, uncle, and mahram by rada' or musahara is conditional on there being no litigation with him. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath) And according to Malik, if they are in his household, it is not accepted; and from him: if much benefit reaches them, not accepted.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage lists relatives for whom a person's testimony is accepted: brother, uncle, parents by rada', wife's mother and daughter, son-in-law, daughter-in-law, father, and people of bid'ah. The reason is absence of suspicion (tuhmah) because their properties and interests are distinct. This is contrasted with testimony for lineal relatives and spouses, which is not accepted due to tuhmah. The acceptance is conditional on no ongoing litigation. Malik's school differs, requiring being out of household or no substantial benefit.

Key Terms
التهمة (al-tuhmah): Suspicion of bias that could affect witness truthfulness.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus; the text claims consensus on acceptance for some relatives.
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Litigation or dispute between parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition is testimony for a brother not accepted in the Hanafi school?
A) Always accepted.
B) If the brother is a witness in his own case.
C) If there is litigation between them.
D) If they live together.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is accepted for specific relatives (brother, uncle, in-laws by rada', etc.) in Hanafi law provided there is no litigation between them, due to the absence of tuhmah.

Testimony of Dhimmis and Harbis: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Ruling on testimony of non-Muslims (dhimmis and harbis) against each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on testimony of dhimmis and harbis, explain the evidence, and compare it to the positions of Malik, Shafi'i, and other schools.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's testimony is accepted on fellow dhimmis, not on Muslims, based on practice of the Salaf.

The testimony of non-Muslims in Islamic courts is a matter of ikhtilaf. The Hanafi school, drawing on early practice, accepts testimony of dhimmis and harbis against others of the same legal status. This lesson examines the ruling, the evidence, and the counter-arguments from other madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State what the Hanafi school rules: testimony of a dhimmi is accepted on another dhimmi (even if different religion) but not on a Muslim; testimony of a harbi (musta'min) is accepted on another harbi but not on a dhimmi.
Note the key terms: dhimmi (permanent resident), harbi/musta'min (temporary visitor).
2
Identify opposing madhhab positions
List the views of Malik and Shafi'i (absolute rejection), Ibn Abi Layla (accept if same religion), and Ahmad (rejection in all cases).
These positions are based on different assessments of dhimmi reliability.
3
Examine the Hanafi evidence
Look at the reported practice: 'Ali stoning Jews on Jewish testimony, and consensus of Salaf. Identify the type of evidence (Qawl Sahabi / practice) and its strength according to Hanafi usul.
The Hanafi school relies on precedent and consensus, not mere analogy.
4
Analyze the counter-argument and response
The opposing view argues dhimmis are fasiq due to kufr, thus unreliable. The Hanafi response: fisq does not necessarily entail lying; historical practice shows acceptance.
This is a dispute over the legal effect of kufr on witness credibility.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi courts, a dhimmi can testify against a dhimmi; in Maliki/Shafi'i courts, such testimony is rejected. This affects litigation involving non-Muslim parties.
The difference is not just theoretical; it affects case outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمي على مثله [ أي وكذا تقبل شهادة الذمي على الذمي مثله وإن اختلفا ملة كاليهود والنصارى. (ط، عيني)] لا على المسلم مطلقا، قيد بالذمي؛ لأن المرتد لا شهادة له، واختلفوا في مرتد على مثله، والأصح عدم قبول، سواء كانت ملة الذميين متحدة أو لا، وقال مالك والشافعي رحمة: لا تقبل شهادة الذمي على أحد؛ لأنه فاسق بأحد أنواع الفسق وبه قال أبو عبد الرحمن ربيعة الرأي، وقال ابن أبي ليلى إن اتفقت مللهم تقبل شهادة بعضهم على بعض، وإن اختلفت لا تقبل ولنا ما روي أنه علا رجم يهوديين بشهادة يهود عليهما بالزنا"، وعن أبي موسى الأشعري الله وجابر بن عبد الله : أنه علي أجاز شهادة النصارى بعضهم على بعض، وعليه إجماع السلف. (عيني، فتح) والحربي على مثله : [ أي وكذا تقبل شهادة المستأمن على مثله؛ لأن له ولاية على مثله. (ط، عيني)] والمراد بالحربي المستأمن؛ لأنه لا يتصور غير المستأمن، فإن الحربي لو دخل دارنا بلا أمان يسترق ولا شهادة للعبد على أحد، وقوله : ولا" على الذمي لأنه لا ولاية له عليه؛ لأن الذمي من أهل ديارنا وتقبل شهادة الذمي على المستأمن؛ لأن الذمي أعلى منه حالاً فجاز أن يجعل له ولاية عليه، وعند أحمد: لا تقبل أصلاً في الكل، وإنما قال: "على مثله"؛ لأنهما إذا كانا عن دارين مختلفين كالإفرنج والحبش لا تجوز شهادة أحدهما على الآخر؛ لانقطاع الولاية بينهما.
And a dhimmi on his like [i.e., also the testimony of a dhimmi on a fellow dhimmi is accepted, even if their religions differ like Jews and Christians. (T, al-'Ayni)] Not on a Muslim at all. It is restricted to dhimmi, because an apostate has no testimony, and they differed about an apostate on his like; the correct view is non-acceptance, whether the religions of the dhimmis are the same or not. Malik and al-Shafi'i said: testimony of a dhimmi on anyone is not accepted, because he is a fasiq of a type of fisq, and Abu Abd al-Rahman Rabi'a al-Ra'y also said that. Ibn Abi Layla said: if their religions agree, their testimony on each other is accepted; if they differ, not accepted. Our evidence: It is narrated that 'Ali stoned two Jews based on the testimony of Jews against them for zina; and from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Jabir ibn 'Abdullah that 'Ali permitted testimony of Christians against each other; and upon this is the consensus of the Salaf. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath) And a harbi on his like [i.e., also the testimony of a musta'min on his like is accepted, because he has authority (wilaya) over his like. (T, al-'Ayni)] By harbi is meant musta'min, because no other is conceivable; if a harbi enters our lands without aman, he is enslaved and a slave has no testimony on anyone. And his statement: nor on a dhimmi, because he has no authority over him; because a dhimmi is from the people of our land. And the testimony of a dhimmi is accepted on a musta'min, because the dhimmi is higher in status, so it is permissible to assign him authority over him. According to Ahmad, it is not accepted at all in any case. He only said 'on his like' because if they are from two different lands, like Franks and Habash, the testimony of one on the other is not valid due to the severance of authority between them.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage deals with testimony of non-Muslims. Hanafis accept testimony of a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) on another dhimmi, even if different religions, but not on a Muslim. This is based on practice of 'Ali and consensus of Salaf. Evidence: 'Ali stoned Jews based on Jewish testimony. Malik and Shafi'i reject entirely because they consider dhimmis as fasiq. Ibn Abi Layla accepts only if same religion. For harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory), the text limits it to musta'min (one with temporary safety) because a harbi without aman is enslaved. A musta'min's testimony is accepted on another musta'min, but not on a dhimmi. This is based on the concept of wilaya (authority). If the two harbis are from different lands, testimony is not accepted.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a pact of protection.
الحربي (al-harbi): A non-Muslim from enemy territory; in context, a musta'min (one with safe conduct).
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A non-Muslim given temporary safe conduct (aman) in Muslim lands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, on whom can a dhimmi testify?
A) On any Muslim.
B) On any non-Muslim.
C) On another dhimmi only.
D) On anyone except his relatives.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony of a dhimmi on another dhimmi and of a musta'min on another musta'min, but never on a Muslim, based on the practice of the Salaf.

Testimony of Ahl al-Hawa: Hanafi Acceptance vs. Other Schools
Ruling on testimony of religious innovators with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting testimony from religious innovators and explain the rationale, and compare it with the positions of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad.

Core Idea
Hanafis accept testimony of religious innovators because piety deters lying, unless the belief reaches kufr or permits perjury.

This passage from Kitab al-Shahada addresses a nuanced question: can a witness whose beliefs are sinful (but not kufr) be considered reliable? The Hanafi school answers yes, citing the witness's religious motivation as a deterrent to falsehood. Other schools disagree, viewing doctrinal error as a defect in moral character. Understanding this dispute clarifies how different madhahib weigh the requirement of 'adalah (integrity) in witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six origins of Ahl al-Hawa
List the six major innovated sects mentioned in al-Nihayah: Jabriyyah, Qadariyyah, Rawafid, Khawarij, Mushabbihah, and Mu'attilah.
Each splits into twelve, totalling 72 sects.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its rationale
The Hanafi position is to accept their testimony. The reason given is that their motive is religious devotion (al-tadayyun), which prevents them from lying.
This distinguishes them from ordinary sinners.
3
Note the opposing views
Malik and al-Shafi'i in one view reject altogether. Ahmad rejects for three specific sects: Qadariyyah, Jahmiyyah, and Rafidiyyah.
4
Understand the exception of Khattabiyyah
The Khattabiyyah (extreme Rafidi sect) are excluded from acceptance because they claimed divinity for Ali and Ja'far and allowed perjury for co-sectarians.
Unanimously rejected by all schools.
5
Apply the condition from al-Dhakhira
Testimony is accepted only if the innovation does not amount to kufr. If the belief makes the person a kafir, testimony is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا الخطابية، والذمي على مثله والحربي على مثله. وفي "النهاية": أن أصول أهل الهوى ستة الجبرية والقدرية والروافض والخوارج والمشبهة والمعطلة، ثم كل واحد منهم يفرق اثنتا عشرة فرقة فيصير مجموع الفرق اثنين وسبعين فرقة، وتقبل شهادتهم مع أنهم فسقة من حيث الاعتقاد؛ لأن الحامل لهم على ذلك إنما هو التدين به وذلك مانع من ارتكاب الكذب. وعند مالك والشافعي في قول لا تقبل، وعند أحمد: لا تقبل لثلاثة قدرية وجهمية ورافضية. وشرط في "الذخيرة" لقبول شهادتهم أن يكون هوى لا يكفر به صاحبه. (عيني، عناية) إلا الخطابية وهم قوم من الروافض ينسبون إلى أبي الخطاب محمد بن أبي وهب الأجدع، وقيل: أحمد بن أبي زينب الأسدي الأجدع الذي كان يقول بإمامة إسماعيل بن أبي جعفر، فلما مات إسماعيل رجعوا إلى القول بإمامة جعفر، وغلوا في القول غلواً كبيراً. وقال في "شرح الأقطع": قوم منسوبون إلى أبي الخطاب، رجل كان بالكوفة، قتله عيسى ابن موسى، وصلبه بالكناسة محلة بالكوفة؛ لأنه كان يزعم أن عليا هو الإله الأكبر، وجعفر الصادق هو الإله الأصغر، وقيل: كانوا يعتقدون أن من ادعى منهم شيئاً على غيره، يجب أن يشهد له بقية شيعته. وذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي: أنهم ضرب من الروافض يجوزون أداء الشهادة إذا حلف المدعي بين أيديهم أنه محق في دعواه، ويقولون: المسلم لا يحلف كاذباً ويدينون بشهادة الزور لموافقيهم على مخالفهم.
Except the Khattabiyyah, and the dhimmi against his like, and the harbi against his like. And in al-Nihayah: The origins of ahl al-hawa are six: al-Jabriyyah, al-Qadariyyah, al-Rawafid, al-Khawarij, al-Mushabbihah, and al-Mu'attilah, then each of them splits into twelve sects, making total sects seventy-two. And their testimony is accepted even though they are sinful in terms of belief, because the motive for them is only religious devotion, and that prevents them from committing lies. According to Malik and al-Shafi'i in one view, it is not accepted. According to Ahmad, it is not accepted for three: Qadariyyah, Jahmiyyah, and Rafidiyyah. And it is conditioned in al-Dhakhira for acceptance of their testimony that it be a whim that does not render its holder an unbeliever. (Ayni, Inayah) Except the Khattabiyyah, and they are a group of the Rawafid attributed to Abu al-Khattab Muhammad bin Abi Wahb al-Ajda', and it is said: Ahmad bin Abi Zaynab al-Asadi al-Ajda' who used to say the imamate of Isma'il bin Abi Ja'far. When Isma'il died, they reverted to the imamate of Ja'far, and they exaggerated greatly in speech. And it is said in Sharh al-Aqta': a group attributed to Abu al-Khattab, a man in Kufa, killed by Isa bin Musa, and crucified at al-Kunasa, a locality in Kufa, because he claimed that 'Ali is the greatest god, and Ja'far al-Sadiq is the lesser god. And it is said: they believed that if one of them claimed something against another, the rest of his sect must testify for him. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned: they are a type of Rawafid who permit giving testimony if the plaintiff swears before them that he is truthful in his claim, and they say: a Muslim does not swear falsely, and they practice false testimony for their supporters against their opponents.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Shahada discusses the testimony of Ahl al-Hawa (people of religious innovation). The author first lists the six major innovated sects (Jabriyya, Qadariyya, Rawafid, Khawarij, Mushabbiha, Mu'attila) and notes that each splits into twelve, totalling seventy-two sects. The Hanafi ruling is that their testimony is accepted despite their sinful beliefs, because their motivation is religious piety, which deters them from lying. This distinguishes them from ordinary sinners. The commentary notes that Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i in one view reject their testimony, and Ahmad rejects it for three specific sects (Qadariyya, Jahmiyya, Rafidiyya). al-Dhakhira conditions acceptance on the deviation not amounting to kufr. The passage then details the exception of Khattabiyya, an extreme Rafidi sect, whose rejection is unanimous due to their extremism (claiming divinity for Ali and Ja'far). The late Imam al-Sarakhsi adds that they allow perjury for co-sectarians, making their testimony unreliable.

Key Terms
أهل الهوى (Ahl al-Hawa): People of religious innovation (bid'ah) who follow their whims in belief but are not necessarily outside Islam.
الخطابية (al-Khattabiyyah): An extreme Rafidi sect attributed to Abu al-Khattab, who claimed divinity for Ali and Ja'far and legalized perjury for co-sectarians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept the testimony of Ahl al-Hawa?
A) Because they are righteous Muslims
B) Because their religious devotion deters them from lying
C) Because the ruler compels them
D) Because they are usually truthful

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony from religious innovators as long as their deviation does not reach kufr or permit perjury, whereas other schools are more restrictive.

Testimony of Dhimmi and Harbi: Acceptance Against Their Likes
Ruling on accepting testimony of dhimmi and harbi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of dhimmis and harbis (musta'mins) and apply the conditions for its acceptance.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's testimony is valid only against another dhimmi; a harbi's only against his like from the same abode.

Testimony (shahada) is a critical means of proof in Islamic law. This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the testimony of non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) and enemy non-Muslims granted safe passage (musta'mins). You will learn the specific conditions under which their testimony is accepted, the evidence from the Prophet's practice, and how this ruling differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the witnesses and accused
Identify the legal status of the witness: dhimmi, harbi (musta'min), or Muslim. Also categorize the accused.
The ruling is symmetrical: testimony is accepted only against one's 'like' in category.
2
State the Hanafi rule for dhimmi testimony
A dhimmi's testimony is accepted against another dhimmi, regardless of their specific religion (Jewish, Christian, etc.), but not against a Muslim.
The restriction excludes apostates; their testimony is rejected entirely.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The proof is the hadith of the Prophet stoning two Jews based on Jewish testimony, and the practice of companions Abu Musa and Jabir allowing Christian testimony against Christians.
This is a fi'l (action) of the Prophet, indicating permissibility.
4
Compare with other schools
Malik and al-Shafi'i reject dhimmi testimony entirely; Ibn Abi Layla accepts it only if religions agree; Ahmad rejects all non-Muslim testimony.
The Hanafi response argues that dhimmis have wilaya (authority) over their own kind, despite being sinners.
5
Apply the rule to harbi (musta'min)
A harbi's testimony is accepted only against another musta'min from the same dar al-harb, not against a dhimmi or Muslim. If the accused converts to Islam before judgment, the testimony becomes invalid.
'Harbi' here means musta'min; a harbi without aman is enslaved and cannot testify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمي على مثله [ أي وكذا تقبل شهادة الذمي على الذمي مثله وإن اختلفا ملة كاليهود والنصارى. (ط، عيني)] لا على المسلم مطلقا، قيد بالذمي؛ لأن المرتد لا شهادة له، واختلفوا في مرتد على مثله، والأصح عدم قبول، سواء كانت ملة الذميين متحدة أو لا، وقال مالك والشافعي رحمة: لا تقبل شهادة الذمي على أحد؛ لأنه فاسق بأحد أنواع الفسق وبه قال أبو عبد الرحمن ربيعة الرأي، وقال ابن أبي ليلى إن اتفقت مللهم تقبل شهادة بعضهم على بعض، وإن اختلفت لا تقبل ولنا ما روي أنه عليه السلام رجم يهوديين بشهادة يهود عليهما بالزنا، وعن أبي موسى الأشعري وجابر بن عبد الله : أنهما أجازا شهادة النصارى بعضهم على بعض، وعليه إجماع السلف. (عيني، فتح)
والحربي على مثله : [ أي وكذا تقبل شهادة المستأمن على مثله؛ لأن له ولاية على مثله. (ط، عيني)] والمراد بالحربي المستأمن؛ لأنه لا يتصور غير المستأمن، فإن الحربي لو دخل دارنا بلا أمان يسترق ولا شهادة للعبد على أحد، وقوله : ولا على الذمي لأنه لا ولاية له عليه؛ لأن الذمي من أهل ديارنا وتقبل شهادة الذمي على المستأمن؛ لأن الذمي أعلى منه حالاً فجاز أن يجعل له ولاية عليه، وعند أحمد: لا تقبل أصلاً في الكل، وإنما قال: "على مثله"؛ لأنهما إذا كانا عن دارين مختلفين كالإفرنج والحبش لا تجوز شهادة أحدهما على الآخر؛ لانقطاع الولاية بينهما، ولهذا لا يتوارثان والدار إنما يختلف باختلاف المنعة والملك بخلاف أهل الذمة حيث تقبل شهادة بعضهم على بعض، وإن كان هذا رومياً وذاك تركياً؛ لأنهما من دارنا، وقال الناصحي فإن أسلم المشهود عليه قبل أن يقضى عليه بطلت الشهادة.
And the dhimmi against his like [meaning: likewise the testimony of a dhimmi against a dhimmi like him is accepted even if their religions differ, like Jews and Christians. (T, Ayni)] Not against a Muslim absolutely; restricted to dhimmi because the apostate has no testimony; they differed regarding an apostate against his like; the most correct is non-acceptance, whether the religion of the dhimmis is the same or not. Malik and al-Shafi'i said: the testimony of a dhimmi is not accepted against anyone because he is a sinner by one type of sin, and Abu Abd al-Rahman Rabi'a al-Ra'y said likewise. Ibn Abi Layla said: if their religions agree, their testimony is accepted against one another; if they differ, it is not accepted. And our proof is what is reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) stoned two Jews based on the testimony of Jews against them for adultery, and from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and Jabir ibn Abd Allah that they allowed the testimony of Christians against one another, and there is consensus of the Salaf upon that. (Ayni, Fath) And the harbi against his like [meaning: likewise the testimony of a musta'min against his like is accepted because he has authority over his like. (T, Ayni)] By 'harbi' is meant musta'min, because a non-musta'min is inconceivable; if a harbi enters our land without aman, he is enslaved and a slave has no testimony. And his saying: not against the dhimmi, because he has no authority over him, because the dhimmi is from the people of our land. And the testimony of a dhimmi against a musta'min is accepted because the dhimmi is higher in status than him, so it is permissible to grant him authority over him. According to Ahmad: it is not accepted at all in all cases. The author said 'against his like' because if they are from two different abodes, like Franks and Abyssinians, testimony of one against the other is not allowed due to severance of authority between them, hence they do not inherit from each other. The abode differs only by difference of protection and sovereignty, unlike the people of dhimma, where testimony of some against others is accepted even if this one is Roman and that one Turkish, because they are from our abode. Al-Nasihi said: if the accused converts to Islam before judgment, the testimony becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Shahada deals with the testimony of dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects of the Islamic state) and harbis (warring non-Muslims, specifically musta'mins). The Hanafi ruling is that a dhimmi's testimony is accepted against another dhimmi, regardless of their specific religion (Jewish, Christian, etc.), but not against a Muslim. The restriction to 'dhimmi' excludes apostates, whose testimony is rejected. The Hanafis argue that dhimmis have legal authority (wilaya) over each other. Their proof includes the hadith where the Prophet stoned two Jews based on Jewish testimony, and the practice of the Companions. Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i reject dhimmi testimony altogether due to their sinfulness. Ibn Abi Layla accepts it only if the religions agree. Similarly, testimony of a harbi (musta'min) is accepted only against another musta'min from the same abode of war, not against a dhimmi or a Muslim. This is because the wilaya between two musta'mins is established by their shared status. Ahmad rejects all such testimony. The passage also notes that if the accused becomes Muslim before judgment, the testimony is invalid. This is explained by al-Nasihi.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizya and enjoys protection.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic rule, typically an enemy. Here specifically the musta'min (one granted aman).
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who has been granted temporary safe passage (aman) into Muslim lands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, against whom is the testimony of a dhimmi accepted?
A) Against any person
B) Only against another dhimmi
C) Only against a Muslim
D) Only against a harbi

Key Takeaway
Testimony of non-Muslims is accepted in Hanafi fiqh only against their own kind, based on prophetic practice and the concept of wilaya.

Testimony of One Who Commits Small Sins
Ruling on accepting testimony of someone who commits minor sins without persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from a person who commits minor sins and explain the definition of 'adalah (moral integrity) using the criterion of predominant good deeds and avoidance of major sins.

Core Idea
A witness's minor sins do not disqualify his testimony as long as good deeds outweigh evil and major sins are avoided.

The Hanafi school recognizes that no human is infallible except prophets. Requiring sinlessness would block the institution of testimony. Instead, 'adalah is defined by a preponderance of good over evil, along with avoidance of major sins. Minor sins (al-lamam) committed without persistence do not undermine a witness's reliability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The testimony of a person who commits minor sins. The ruling: his testimony is accepted (mubah) if conditions are met.
The ruling is permissive, not obligatory.
2
Define 'adalah from the text
The text defines the just witness ('adil) as one whose good deeds outweigh evil deeds and who avoids all major sins.
This is a practical, attainable standard.
3
Distinguish minor vs major sins
Minor sins (al-lamam) are those below major sins (kaba'ir). The key is persistence: a minor sin without persistence does not harm 'adalah.
Persistence can elevate a minor sin to a major one.
4
Apply the 'dominant quality' principle
After ensuring avoidance of major sins, the prevailing quality (good or bad) is considered. If good deeds dominate, testimony is accepted.
A single major sin without persistence does not permanently disqualify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ألم بصغيرة: أي أذنب بمعصية صغيرة، مشتق من اللمم وهو الصغيرة ما دون الفواحش وكانت حسناته سيئاته تقبل شهادته؛ لأن الإلمام من غير إصرار لا يقدح في العدالة؛ إذ لا يوجد من البشر من هو معصوم سوى الأنبياء فيؤدي اشتراط العصمة إلى سد باب الشهادة، وهو مفتوح، ثم الصحيح في حد العدالة المعتبرة أن العادل من غلبت حسناته على سيئاته، ولابد من الاجتناب عن الكبائر كلها، ثم بعد ذلك يعتبر الغالب، فلو ارتكب كبيرة مرة قبلت شهادته؛ لعدم الإصرار عليها.
And whoever commits a small sin: i.e., sinned by a minor disobedience, derived from al-lamam which is minor sin beneath the major sins. If his good deeds outweigh his evil deeds, his testimony is accepted, because committing a sin without persistence does not detract from 'adalah (moral integrity) since no human being is infallible except the prophets; requiring infallibility would close the door of testimony, which is open. Then the correct definition of considered 'adalah is that the just person is one whose good deeds outweigh his evil deeds. It is necessary to avoid all major sins. Then afterwards, the dominant [quality] is considered. So if he committed a major sin once, his testimony is accepted due to lack of persistence upon it.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the testimony of one who commits minor sins (al-lamam). The author defines 'alima bi saghira' as committing a small sin, derived from 'lamam' (minor sin below major sins). The ruling is that such a person's testimony is accepted if his good deeds outweigh his bad deeds, provided he avoids major sins. This is because committing a minor sin without persistence does not harm 'adalah (moral integrity). The author argues that requiring infallibility would make testimony impossible, as only prophets are infallible. The correct definition of 'adalah is predominance of good over evil. Even a single major sin does not permanently disqualify if not persisted in. Thus, a person who repents or does not persist is still admissible.

Key Terms
اللمم (al-lamam): Minor sins, as opposed to major sins (kaba'ir). Derived from Quran: إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُمْ سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ.
عدالة ('adalah): Moral integrity required for a witness; defined as predominance of good over evil and avoidance of major sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi definition of a just witness ('adil) regarding sins?
A) One who commits no sins at all
B) One whose good deeds outweigh evil deeds and avoids major sins
C) One who has repented from all sins
D) One who performs all obligatory acts

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a witness's testimony is accepted as long as good deeds outweigh evil and major sins are avoided; minor sins without persistence do not impair 'adalah.

Testimony of Individuals with Physical or Birth Conditions: Aqlaf, Khasi, Walad al-Zina, and Khuntha
Rulings on four witness types expanded from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of each of the four categories (aqlaf, khasi, walad al-zina, khuntha) and identify the conditions for acceptance.

Core Idea
Circumcision, eunuch status, illegitimacy, or ambiguous gender do not inherently disqualify a witness—'adalah is the measure.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses whether certain physical or birth conditions affect a person's capacity to testify. The commentators clarify that the key requirement for testimony is moral integrity ('adalah), not these external factors. We will examine each category's ruling and the reasoning behind the Hanafi school's inclusive approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify each category and the potential objection
For each of the four (aqlaf, khasi, walad al-zina, khuntha), note what might cause someone to doubt the witness's qualification.
E.g., aqlaf: lack of circumcision; khasi: missing testicles; walad al-zina: sinful origin; khuntha: ambiguous gender.
2
State the default Hanafi ruling
For each, determine whether the testimony is accepted or rejected, and under what conditions.
Aqlaf: accepted unless abandonment out of contempt; khasi: accepted if 'adil; walad al-zina: accepted absolutely; khuntha: accepted if unambiguous, else special rules.
3
Examine the commentary's reasoning and proofs
Review the textual proofs and analogies used to support the rulings, including references to Ibn Abbas and the response to Malik.
Focus on the argument that physical condition does not affect 'adalah.
4
Note intra- and cross-madhhab differences
Identify where Hanafi differs from other views, especially regarding walad al-zina and the interpretation of Ibn Abbas's statement.
Malik rejects walad al-zina's testimony in zina cases; Hanafi accepts.
5
Apply the ruling to specific sub-rules
For the ambiguous khuntha, apply the rules for non-hadd and hadd cases, and for the aqlaf who can circumcise himself.
Ambiguous khuntha is treated as a woman for testimony; aqlaf must circumcise if able.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقلف فيه لغتان الأقلف بالقاف، والأغلف بالغين، وهو الذي لم يختن وإنما تقبل شهادته؛ لإطلاق النصوص من غير قيد بالختان والختان سنة للرجال مكرمة للنساء؛ إذ جماع المختونة ألذ وعن ابن عباس له: أنه لا تقبل شهادة الأقلف، وهو محمول على ما إذا تركه استخفافاً بالسنة والدين، وإذا تركه لعذر به من كبر أو خوف هلاك لا يضر عدالته، وقال الناصحي تجوز شهادة الأقلف إذا كان عدلاً، ولم يقدر للختان وقت معين، ولم يرد بذلك نص ولا إجماع، والمتأخرون قدروه من سبع سنين إلى عشر وبعضهم اليوم السابع عن ولادته، والأقلف الكبير إذا أمكنه أن يختن نفسه فعل وإلا عليه أن يتزوج أو يشتري ختانة فتختنه، وقال في البحر: يختنه الحمامي. (عيني، فتح، عناية) والخصي: أي وكذا تقبل شهادة منزوع الخصيتين إذا كان عدلا . (عيني) وولد الزنا: التحقق العدالة منهما؛ لأن قطع العضو أو زيادته أو جناية أبويه لا يوجب قدحا في العدالة. (عيني)] أي تقبل شهادته مطلقاً، سواء شهد في الزنا أو لا، وقال مالك: تقبل في جميع الحقوق إلا في الزنا؛ لأنه يجب أن يكون غيره مثله، قلنا: الكلام في العدل وحبه ذلك بقلبه ليس بقادح؛ لأنه غير مؤاخذ به ما لم يتحدث به؛ ولأن العدل لا يختار ذلك. والخنثى: إن لم يكن مشكلاً، فلا إشكال؛ لأنه رجل أو امرأة وشهادة المخنثين مقبولة بالنص، وإن كان مشكلاً لا يشهد مع رجل ولا مع امرأة ولو شهد يجعل امرأة في حق الشهادة احتياطاً، حتى لا يجوز أن يشهد مع رجل ما لم يضم إليه امرأة أخرى، ولا مع النساء بلا رجل معين، وهذا في غير الحدود، وأما في الحدود، فلا تقبل شهادته، لاحتمال أن يكون امرأة. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And al-aqlaf: there are two dialects: al-aqlaf with qaf, and al-aghiaf with ghain; it is the one who has not been circumcised. His testimony is accepted because the texts are absolute without restriction of circumcision. Circumcision is sunnah for men and an honor for women; intercourse with a circumcised woman is more pleasurable. From Ibn Abbas: he said the testimony of an aqlaf is not accepted, but this is interpreted as when he abandons it out of contempt for the sunnah and religion. If he abandons it due to an excuse like old age or fear of harm, it does not harm his 'adalah. Al-Nasihi said: the testimony of an aqlaf is permissible if he is 'adil; no specific time has been set for circumcision, and there is no text or consensus on it. The later scholars estimated it from seven to ten years, and some say the seventh day after birth. An old aqlaf, if he can circumcise himself, should do so; otherwise he must marry or buy a circumciser to circumcise him. It is said in al-Bahr: the bathhouse attendant circumcises him. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) And al-khasi: meaning likewise the testimony of one whose testicles are removed is accepted if he is 'adil. (Ayni) And walad al-zina: 'adalah is verified from them, because removal of a limb or its addition or the offense of his parents does not cause defect in 'adalah. (Ayni) Meaning his testimony is accepted absolutely, whether he testifies regarding zina or not. Malik said: it is accepted in all rights except in zina, because it is necessary that another be like him. We reply: the matter is about the just person; his love of that in his heart is not detrimental because he is not held accountable for it unless he speaks; and because the just person does not choose that. And al-khuntha: if it is not ambiguous, there is no issue; he is either a man or a woman. The testimony of mukhannaths is accepted by text. If it is ambiguous, he does not testify with a man nor with a woman. If he testifies, he is treated as a woman for testimony out of caution, so it is not permissible for him to testify with a man unless another woman is added, nor with women without a specific man. This is in non-hadd cases. In hadd cases, his testimony is not accepted due to the possibility that he is a woman. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah)
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses the testimony of four categories of people: the uncircumcised (aqlaf), the eunuch (khasi), the child of adultery (walad al-zina), and the hermaphrodite (khuntha). 
1. Aqlaf: The uncircumcised male's testimony is generally accepted because circumcision is a sunnah, not a condition for 'adalah. The report from Ibn Abbas rejecting it is interpreted as applying only when leaving circumcision out of disdain. If there is a valid excuse (e.g., medical), 'adalah is preserved. 
2. Khasi: A eunuch's testimony is accepted if he is otherwise 'adil, as removal of testicles does not affect moral integrity. 
3. Walad al-zina: The testimony of an illegitimate child is accepted absolutely, even in cases of zina, because his parents' sin does not taint his 'adalah. Imam Malik's exception for zina testimony is refuted: the witness's internal desire does not disqualify him. 
4. Khuntha: A hermaphrodite's testimony is accepted if the sex is clear. If ambiguous (mushkil), he/she cannot testify alone or with a man or woman without caution. In non-hadd cases, the hermaphrodite is treated as a woman, so testimony requires another female or a male, etc. In hadd cases, testimony is rejected due to uncertainty of gender. The ruling is based on caution (ihtiyat).

Key Terms
أقلف (aqlaf): An uncircumcised man.
خصي (khasi): A eunuch, one whose testicles have been removed.
ولد الزنا (walad al-zina): A child born out of wedlock.
خنثى (khuntha): A hermaphrodite, a person with ambiguous gender characteristics.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is the testimony of an ambiguous hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil) treated in non-hadd cases according to the Hanafi school?
A) His testimony is rejected entirely
B) He is treated as a man
C) He is treated as a woman, so requires joining another woman or man as per rules
D) He may testify alone if he is 'adil

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds that physical conditions or birth status do not determine witness capacity; moral integrity ('adalah) is the sole criterion.

Shahadat — Testimony

Conditions for Acceptance of Testimony: Justice and Physical Status
Chapter on whose testimony is accepted, with conditions and inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of legal justice (al-ʿadāla) and explain why physical or birth defects do not disqualify a witness, using the examples of the uncircumcised, eunuch, and child of fornication.

Core Idea
Witness credibility rests on religious justice (adala), not physical perfection or lineage.

The chapter on witness acceptance lists individuals who might seem disqualified due to physical state or birth, but the Hanafi school emphasizes that justice is determined by religious commitment and moral conduct. This lesson examines the conditions for testimony, the definition of justice, and the rulings for specific categories, including a key inter-madhhab dispute over the child of fornication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational condition
Determine the Hanafi definition of justice (al-ʿadāla) as stated in the text: one whose good deeds outweigh bad deeds and who avoids major sins. Note that a single major sin without persistence does not disqualify.
Justice is not perfection; it is the predominant state of piety.
2
Classify the listed categories
List the categories: the dhimmi (testimony not accepted against Muslims), the one who commits minor sins, the uncircumcised, the eunuch, the child of fornication, and the hermaphrodite. For each, identify the potential defect that might raise doubt.
The dhimmi category is an exception: their testimony against Muslims is rejected, but among themselves it is accepted.
3
Apply the definition of justice
For each category (except dhimmi), show that the apparent defect does not affect justice. For the uncircumcised: circumcision is sunnah, not a condition. For eunuch: cutting a limb does not affect justice. For child of fornication: lineage does not affect justice.
The text cites absolute texts (mutilaq al-nusus) as evidence for the aqlaf ruling.
4
Analyze the dispute on walad al-zina
Imam Malik holds that a child of fornication cannot testify in zina cases due to possible bias. The Hanafi response: internal liking is not a legal defect; a just person does not act on such inclinations.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (al-mukhtalaf fih).
5
Determine the ruling for the ambiguous hermaphrodite
If the hermaphrodite is not ambiguous (mushkil), testimony is accepted as per text. If ambiguous, he is treated as a woman for testimony: cannot testify with a man unless joined by another woman, and not in hudud cases.
This is a precautionary ruling due to uncertainty.

(عيني، فتح، عناية)
Chapter on whose testimony is accepted. Not upon the dhimmi, and whoever commits a minor sin if he avoids major sins, and the uncircumcised, the eunuch, the child of fornication, and the hermaphrodite. ... And from it they do not inherit from each other, and the house differs according to protection and ownership, unlike the people of dhimma where the testimony of some of them is accepted against others, even if this one is Roman and that one is Turkish, because they are from our territory. And al-Nasihi said: If the one testified against becomes Muslim before the judgment is passed, the testimony is invalidated. ... Commentary on the uncircumcised: there are two pronunciations: al-aqlaf with qaf and al-aghlaaf with ghayn, and it is one who has not been circumcised. His testimony is accepted because of the absolute texts without restriction by circumcision, and circumcision is a sunnah for men and a favor for women because intercourse with a circumcised woman is more pleasurable. And from Ibn Abbas: that the testimony of an uncircumcised man is not accepted, and this is interpreted as when he abandons it out of disrespect for the sunnah and religion. If he leaves it for a valid excuse such as old age or fear of death, it does not harm his justice. Al-Nasihi said: the testimony of an uncircumcised person is permissible if he is just, and no specific time is set for circumcision, nor is there any text or consensus on it. The later scholars estimated it from seven to ten years, some from the seventh day after birth. If an adult uncircumcised man can circumcise himself, he must; otherwise he must marry or buy a female circumciser to circumcise him. Al-Bahr says: the bathhouse attendant circumcises him. ... And the eunuch: similarly the testimony of one with removed testicles is accepted if he is just. And the child of fornication: verification of justice from them, because cutting off a limb or its excess or the crime of his parents does not cause a defect in justice. It means his testimony is accepted absolutely, whether he testifies in zina or not. And Malik said: accepted in all rights except in zina because he would like that others are like him. We said: the discussion is about the just person, and his liking that in his heart is not a defect because he is not accountable for it as long as he does not speak it, and because the just person does not choose that. And for the hermaphrodite: if not ambiguous, there is no issue because he is a man or a woman, and the testimony of hermaphrodites is accepted by text. If ambiguous, he does not testify with a man nor with a woman, and if he testifies, he is made a woman in the matter of testimony as a precaution, so it is not permissible to testify with a man unless another woman is added, nor with women without a specific man. This is in non-hudud cases. As for hudud, his testimony is not accepted due to the possibility of being a woman.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq opens the chapter on whose testimony is accepted. The matn lists several categories: the dhimmi (testimony not accepted against a Muslim, but among themselves it is accepted even if from different ethnicities), the one who commits minor sins if he avoids major sins (the Hanafi position that justice is determined by overwhelming good deeds, not by perfection), the uncircumcised (aqlaf) (testimony accepted because circumcision is sunnah, not a condition of justice; Ibn Abbas's contrary view is interpreted as referring to someone who abandons it out of disrespect), the eunuch (khasi) (accepted if just), the child of fornication (walad al-zina) (accepted absolutely; Malik's objection that he might be biased in zina cases is rejected because internal feelings are not a legal defect unless expressed), and the hermaphrodite (khuntha) (if not ambiguous, testimony accepted; if ambiguous, treated as a woman for testimony purposes, so must be accompanied by another woman in non-hudud cases, and not accepted in hudud). The commentary explains each with references to other works like al-Bahr and al-Urf.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-ʿadāla): Legal uprightness required for witness acceptance; in this text, defined as one whose good deeds outweigh his bad deeds, and who avoids major sins.
الأقلف (al-aqlaf): Uncircumcised man; his testimony is accepted because circumcision is sunnah, not a condition of justice.
ولد الزنا (walad al-zinā): Child born of fornication; his testimony is accepted absolutely.
الخصي (al-khaṣī): Eunuch (one with removed testicles); his testimony is accepted if just.
الخنثى (al-khunthā): Hermaphrodite; if not ambiguous, testimony accepted; if ambiguous, treated as a woman for testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under what condition is the testimony of a person who commits minor sins accepted?
A) He must perform tawba (repentance) for each minor sin.
B) He avoids major sins and his good deeds outweigh his bad deeds.
C) He must be circumcised and free from all sins.
D) He must not have committed any minor sin in the past month.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, witness testimony is accepted based on religious justice (adala), not physical perfection or birth; minor sins do not disqualify if major sins are avoided.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony of Workers and Emancipators
Ruling on acceptance of testimony of workers and emancipators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of workers and emancipators and identify the conditions for its acceptance.

Core Idea
Occupation alone does not disqualify testimony; only injustice or lack of integrity does.

This lesson covers two categories of witnesses whose testimony is sometimes questioned: workers (tax collectors) and emancipators (masters and freed slaves). The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that the Hanafi school accepts their testimony provided they are just and free from disqualifying traits, countering assumptions that their roles automatically create suspicion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
The passage discusses three types: 'ummal (workers/sultans' agents), mu'tiq (emancipator), and mu'taq (freed slave).
Workers are defined as those who collect obligatory dues like kharaj and jizya.
2
Understand the condition of 'adalah
The central condition is that the witness must be just (adl). Their occupation alone is not a disqualifier; only engaging in oppression or fisq is.
The passage quotes Abu al-Layth comparing workers to Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (acceptable) vs. Yazid ibn Muawiya (not acceptable).
3
Note the later disagreement
Some later Hanafis argued that due to widespread injustice, workers' testimony should be rejected outright. However, the text upholds the original view: if they are just, it is accepted.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is acceptance conditioned on justice.
4
Apply the principle to emancipators
The testimony between emancipator and freed slave is accepted without dispute because there is no suspicion (tuhma) between them.
This is based on lack of benefit or harm from the testimony.

(عيني) ولو شهدا أي لو مات رجل وترك ابنين فشهدا (عيني والوصي يدعي إلخ وهو الرجل المذكور، والواو للحال. (عيني)] المراد من الدعوى هنا الرضاء؛ إذ الجواز لا يتوقف على الدعوى، بل للقاضي أن ينصب وصياً إذا رضي به جاز أي شهادة الابنين وتذكير الفعل باعتبار المذكور من الشهادة، وهذا الجواز استحسان، والقياس أن لا تقبل؛ لأن الشاهد لنفسه نفعاً، وجه الاستحسان: أن للقاضي ولاية نصب الوصي، وهذه الشهادة كفته مؤونة التعيين، لا أنه ثبت بها شيء لم يكن للقاضي فعله، بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن الموت ظاهراً؛ لأنه حينئذ لا يملك القاضي نصب الوصي إلا بهذه البينة وفيها تهمة لجر النفع إلى الشاهد. وإن أنكر :لا أي إن أنكر الرجل الوصية لا تقبل شهادتهما؛ لأن القاضي لا يجبر أحداً على قبول الوصية، والحاصل أن شهادة الابنين في صورة رضاء الوصي يجوز؛ لأنها ليست بشهادة حقيقة؛ إذ الشهادة الحقيقية توجب على القاضي ما لا يتمكن منه ،بدونها، وهذه ليست كذلك ؛ لتمكنه من نصب الوصي إذا رضي الوصي، والموت معروف؛ حفظاً لأموال الناس، لكن عليه أن يتأمل في صلاحية من ينصبه، وبهذه الشهادة كفى مؤونة التعيين، ولم يثبت الشهود بها شيئاً آخر، فصارت كالقرعة في كونها ليست بحجة، بل هي واقعة لمؤونته تعيين القاضي. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
كما لو شهدا : تمثيل لعدم قبول الشهادة في صورة إنكار الوصي بمسألة أخرى أي كما لا تجوز شهادة الابنين في غيبة أبيهما لو شهدا أن أباهما الغائب وكل فلاناً بقبض ديونه، وهذا القيد اتفاقي، إذ لو شهدا أن أباهما وكله بالخصومة لم تقبل أيضاً، والمراد بالابنين كل من لا تقبل شهادته للموكل، وإنما لا تقبل هذه الشهادة،
And the workers, the emancipator and the freed slave. And if two (sons) testify that their father made a bequest to him, and the executor (wasi) claims (accepts), it is permissible. And if he denies, it is not, just as if they testified that their father appointed an agent to collect his debts... Commentary on workers: the testimony of workers is also accepted - with damm on 'ayn and shaddah on mim - plural of 'amil. They are the sultans' collectors of obligatory dues like kharaj, jizya, and sadaqat according to the majority. They said: this was in their era; in our time their testimony is not accepted due to prevalence of oppression. The summary is that if they are just, it is accepted, otherwise not. Some said workers means rulers; some said they are those who work with their hands and hire themselves out. In any case, their testimony is accepted because the work itself is not fisq, unless they are assistants to oppression, then their testimony is not accepted. It is said if the worker is dignified, with good character, does not speak rashly, does not often swear, and does not habitually curse his children or others, his testimony is accepted; otherwise not. Abu al-Layth said: if the worker is like Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz in his religiosity, his testimony is permissible; if like Yazid ibn Muawiya, it is not accepted. And the emancipator: similarly the testimony of the emancipator to the freed slave and vice versa is accepted due to lack of suspicion. And if two sons testify... meaning a man died leaving two sons, they testify that their father made a bequest to so-and-so, and the executor (the man being discussed) claims (i.e., accepts), then it is permissible. The verb 'jaza' is masculine because shahada is implied. This permissibility is istihsan; by qiyas it would not be accepted because the witness benefits for himself. The basis of istihsan: the judge has authority to appoint an executor, and this testimony saves him the trouble of identification, not that it establishes something the judge could not do. Contrary to when death is not apparent; then the judge cannot appoint an executor except through this evidence, which is suspect due to benefit to the witness. If the executor denies, their testimony is not accepted because the judge cannot force anyone to accept an executorship. In summary, the testimony of the two sons in the case of the executor's consent is permissible because it is not a true testimony; true testimony obliges the judge to do something he cannot do without it. This is not so because he can appoint an executor if the executor consents and death is known. The judge must thus assess the suitability of the appointee; this testimony merely facilitates identification, not proof of right, like casting lots. The example: just as testimony is not accepted when the executor denies, it is also not accepted if the two sons testify in the absence of their father that he appointed an agent to collect debts. This condition is agreed upon; if they testified that their father appointed an agent for litigation, it is also not accepted. The term 'two sons' refers to anyone whose testimony is not accepted for the principal.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on accepted testimony. It first addresses workers ('ummal): the term refers to agents of rulers who collect obligatory dues. While some later scholars argued that due to prevalent oppression such witnesses are no longer accepted, the Hanafi position is that their testimony is accepted if they are just; the work itself is not a disqualifier. Distinctions are drawn based on character and religiosity. Next, the testimony between emancipator and freed slave is accepted because there is no suspicion between them. Then a detailed scenario: two sons testify that their father appointed a certain person as executor (wasi) for his will. If the executor accepts (i.e., agrees to the role), the testimony is accepted by istihsan, even though qiyas would reject it because the sons benefit (they are relieved of responsibility). The istihsan reasoning is that the judge already has authority to appoint an executor; the testimony merely identifies a willing candidate, saving the judge the effort. However, if the executor denies, the testimony is not accepted because the judge cannot compel acceptance. A comparison is drawn with a case where sons testify that their absent father appointed an agent to collect debts; such testimony is also not accepted because it would give the sons a benefit (control over the debt collection). The underlying principle is that testimony that merely assists the judge in exercising his existing authority is acceptable, while testimony that creates a right which the judge could not establish without it is not, due to the potential for bias.

Key Terms
العمال (al-ʿummāl): Workers, specifically sultans' agents who collect taxes and dues. Their testimony is accepted if they are just.
المعتق (al-muʿtiq): The emancipator, i.e., the master who frees a slave.
المُعتق (al-muʿtaq): The freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for the testimony of a worker to be accepted?
A) He must be appointed by a just ruler.
B) He must not curse his children habitually.
C) He must collect only zakat, not kharaj.
D) He must be a government employee.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a worker's occupation does not disqualify him from testifying; only his personal justice and character matter.

Shahadat — Testimony

Sons' Testimony About a Will: Qiyas and Istihsan
Ruling on sons' testimony in will and agency cases, with qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on sons' testimony regarding their father's bequest and distinguish between the qiyas ruling and the istihsan exception.

Core Idea
Sons' testimony about a will is accepted by istihsan only when the executor accepts; otherwise, it is rejected by qiyas.

This lesson examines a detailed scenario where two sons testify that their father appointed a specific executor for his will. Strict analogy (qiyas) would reject the testimony because the sons benefit from it. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan to accept the testimony if the executor consents. The reasoning is that the judge already has authority to appoint an executor; the testimony merely identifies a willing candidate, saving the judge effort. The passage also contrasts this with a case involving agency, which is always rejected.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A man dies leaving two sons. They testify that their father appointed a specific person (say Zayd) as executor (wasi) for his will. Zayd accepts the role.
The term 'wasi' means executor; the father's will is implied.
2
Identify the qiyas ruling and its illa
By qiyas, the testimony is not accepted. The illa is that the witness derives a benefit (naf'): relieving themselves of the responsibility of executing the will.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) because the direct benefit is apparent.
3
Identify the istihsan ruling and its basis
The testimony is accepted by istihsan. The basis: the judge has the authority (wilaya) to appoint an executor independently. This testimony only identifies a willing person, not establishing a new right. It is like casting lots—a procedural aid, not a proof.
Istihsan overrides qiyas because the judge's authority makes the testimony non-essential.
4
Note the condition for acceptance
The executor must accept (yad'ī). If he denies or is unknown, the testimony is not accepted because the judge cannot force acceptance.
The text says 'wa al-wasi yad'ī jāz' meaning if the executor claims (accepts), it is permissible.
5
Compare with the agency case
If the sons testify that their absent father appointed an agent to collect debts, the testimony is not accepted even if the agent accepts, because the father is alive and the testimony would give the sons control over his affairs.
This case is clearly distinguished: in the will case, the father is dead, so the judge's authority is more direct.

Source Text

فأما
والحاصل أنهم إن كانوا عدولاً تقبل وإلا لا، وقيل: المراد بالعمال الأمراء، وقيل : هم الذين يعملون بأيديهم ويؤجرون أنفسهم، وأيا ما كان تقبل شهادتهم؛ لأن نفس العمل ليس بفسق، اللهم إلا إذا كانوا أعواناً على الظلم، فحينئذ لا تقبل شهادتهم وقيل إن كان العامل وجيها ذا مروءة ولا يجازف في كلامه ولا يحلف عليه كثيرا ولا اعتاد شتم أولاده أو غيرهم تقبل شهادته وإلا لا وقال الفقيه أبو الليث إن كان العامل مثل عمر بن
يجر
عبد العزيز ونحوه من حيث الديانة تجوز شهادته، وإن كان مثل يزيد بن معاوية ونحوه لا تقبل. (عيني، فتح) والمعتق: أي وكذا تقبل شهادة المعتق - بفتح التاء - للمعتق - بكسر التاء - وكذا بالعكس؛ لعدم التهمة. (عيني) ولو شهدا أي لو مات رجل وترك ابنين فشهدا (عيني والوصي يدعي إلخ وهو الرجل المذكور، والواو للحال. (عيني)] المراد من الدعوى هنا الرضاء؛ إذ الجواز لا يتوقف على الدعوى، بل للقاضي أن ينصب وصياً إذا رضي به جاز أي شهادة الابنين وتذكير الفعل باعتبار المذكور من الشهادة، وهذا الجواز استحسان، والقياس أن لا تقبل؛ لأن الشاهد لنفسه نفعاً، وجه الاستحسان: أن للقاضي ولاية نصب الوصي، وهذه الشهادة كفته مؤونة التعيين، لا أنه ثبت بها شيء لم يكن للقاضي فعله، بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن الموت ظاهراً؛ لأنه حينئذ لا يملك القاضي نصب الوصي إلا بهذه البينة وفيها تهمة لجر النفع إلى الشاهد. وإن أنكر :لا أي إن أنكر الرجل الوصية لا تقبل شهادتهما؛ لأن القاضي لا يجبر أحداً على قبول الوصية، والحاصل أن شهادة الابنين في صورة رضاء الوصي يجوز؛ لأنها ليست بشهادة حقيقة؛ إذ الشهادة الحقيقية توجب على القاضي ما لا يتمكن منه ،بدونها، وهذه ليست كذلك ؛ لتمكنه من نصب الوصي إذا رضي الوصي، والموت معروف؛ حفظاً لأموال الناس، لكن عليه أن يتأمل في صلاحية من ينصبه، وبهذه الشهادة كفى مؤونة التعيين، ولم يثبت الشهود بها شيئاً آخر، فصارت كالقرعة في كونها ليست بحجة، بل هي واقعة لمؤونته تعيين القاضي. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
كما لو شهدا : تمثيل لعدم قبول الشهادة في صورة إنكار الوصي بمسألة أخرى أي كما لا تجوز شهادة الابنين في غيبة أبيهما لو شهدا أن أباهما الغائب وكل فلاناً بقبض ديونه، وهذا القيد اتفاقي، إذ لو شهدا أن أباهما وكله بالخصومة لم تقبل أيضاً، والمراد بالابنين كل من لا تقبل شهادته للموكل، وإنما لا تقبل هذه الشهادة،
And the workers, the emancipator and the freed slave. And if two (sons) testify that their father made a bequest to him, and the executor (wasi) claims (accepts), it is permissible. And if he denies, it is not, just as if they testified that their father appointed an agent to collect his debts... Commentary on workers: the testimony of workers is also accepted - with damm on 'ayn and shaddah on mim - plural of 'amil. They are the sultans' collectors of obligatory dues like kharaj, jizya, and sadaqat according to the majority. They said: this was in their era; in our time their testimony is not accepted due to prevalence of oppression. The summary is that if they are just, it is accepted, otherwise not. Some said workers means rulers; some said they are those who work with their hands and hire themselves out. In any case, their testimony is accepted because the work itself is not fisq, unless they are assistants to oppression, then their testimony is not accepted. It is said if the worker is dignified, with good character, does not speak rashly, does not often swear, and does not habitually curse his children or others, his testimony is accepted; otherwise not. Abu al-Layth said: if the worker is like Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz in his religiosity, his testimony is permissible; if like Yazid ibn Muawiya, it is not accepted. And the emancipator: similarly the testimony of the emancipator to the freed slave and vice versa is accepted due to lack of suspicion. And if two sons testify... meaning a man died leaving two sons, they testify that their father made a bequest to so-and-so, and the executor (the man being discussed) claims (i.e., accepts), then it is permissible. The verb 'jaza' is masculine because shahada is implied. This permissibility is istihsan; by qiyas it would not be accepted because the witness benefits for himself. The basis of istihsan: the judge has authority to appoint an executor, and this testimony saves him the trouble of identification, not that it establishes something the judge could not do. Contrary to when death is not apparent; then the judge cannot appoint an executor except through this evidence, which is suspect due to benefit to the witness. If the executor denies, their testimony is not accepted because the judge cannot force anyone to accept an executorship. In summary, the testimony of the two sons in the case of the executor's consent is permissible because it is not a true testimony; true testimony obliges the judge to do something he cannot do without it. This is not so because he can appoint an executor if the executor consents and death is known. The judge must thus assess the suitability of the appointee; this testimony merely facilitates identification, not proof of right, like casting lots. The example: just as testimony is not accepted when the executor denies, it is also not accepted if the two sons testify in the absence of their father that he appointed an agent to collect debts. This condition is agreed upon; if they testified that their father appointed an agent for litigation, it is also not accepted. The term 'two sons' refers to anyone whose testimony is not accepted for the principal.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on accepted testimony. It first addresses workers ('ummal): the term refers to agents of rulers who collect obligatory dues. While some later scholars argued that due to prevalent oppression such witnesses are no longer accepted, the Hanafi position is that their testimony is accepted if they are just; the work itself is not a disqualifier. Distinctions are drawn based on character and religiosity. Next, the testimony between emancipator and freed slave is accepted because there is no suspicion between them. Then a detailed scenario: two sons testify that their father appointed a certain person as executor (wasi) for his will. If the executor accepts (i.e., agrees to the role), the testimony is accepted by istihsan, even though qiyas would reject it because the sons benefit (they are relieved of responsibility). The istihsan reasoning is that the judge already has authority to appoint an executor; the testimony merely identifies a willing candidate, saving the judge the effort. However, if the executor denies, the testimony is not accepted because the judge cannot compel acceptance. A comparison is drawn with a case where sons testify that their absent father appointed an agent to collect debts; such testimony is also not accepted because it would give the sons a benefit (control over the debt collection). The underlying principle is that testimony that merely assists the judge in exercising his existing authority is acceptable, while testimony that creates a right which the judge could not establish without it is not, due to the potential for bias.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Preferring a different ruling over qiyas due to a stronger reason, such as necessity or easier practice. Here, the testimony of sons is accepted by istihsan despite qiyas.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor of a will, appointed by the testator to carry out the will. In this passage, his acceptance is crucial for the sons' testimony to be valid.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning by which the ruling of a new case is derived from a similar existing case based on a common cause (illa). Here, the qiyas would reject the testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is the testimony of two sons regarding their father's bequest accepted?
A) The father must have written a will.
B) The executor must accept the role.
C) The sons must be the only heirs.
D) The judge must personally know the executor.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi istihsan allows sons' testimony about a will when the executor accepts, based on the judge's existing authority to appoint executors.

Acceptance of Testimony of Workers and Emancipator
Explains the ruling on accepting testimony of workers and emancipator/emancipated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of 'ummal (workers) and mu'tiq (emancipator) and identify the condition that governs its acceptance.

Core Idea
Testimony of a worker is accepted if he is upright; testimony between emancipator and emancipated is always accepted.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on accepting the testimony of two categories: workers of the state and the emancipator/freed slave relationship. The core principle is that testimony hinges on 'adala (uprightness), not occupation or status. We will explore the various opinions within the school and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the passage covers 'ummal (workers, especially state tax collectors) and mu'tiq/mu'taq (emancipator and emancipated).
2
Understand the governing principle
The Hanafi principle is that testimony is accepted based on 'adala (uprightness). For 'ummal, acceptance depends on their justice; for mu'tiq/mu'taq, it is unconditional due to lack of suspicion (tuhma).
3
Note the intra-school ikhtilaf on 'ummal
Early scholars accepted testimony of all 'ummal; later scholars restricted it due to prevalence of oppression. The passage records several views but emphasizes the condition of 'adala.
This is an example of changing fatwa based on changing circumstances (taghayyur al-fatwa bi-taghayyur al-zaman).
4
Apply to a scenario
Take a concrete case: a tax collector known for piety testifies. Apply the ruling: his testimony is accepted. If he is oppressive, it is rejected. For mu'tiq, a master testifying for his freed slave is always accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعمال: أي وكذا تقبل شهادة العمال - بضم العين وتشديد الميم - جمع عامل. وهم عمال السلاطين الذين يأخذون الحقوق الواجبة كالخراج والجزية والصدقات عند الجمهور، وقالوا: هذا كان في عصر هم، في زمننا فلا تقبل شهادتهم؛ لغلبة ظلمهم. فأما والحاصل أنهم إن كانوا عدولاً تقبل وإلا لا، وقيل: المراد بالعمال الأمراء، وقيل: هم الذين يعملون بأيديهم ويؤجرون أنفسهم، وأيا ما كان تقبل شهادتهم؛ لأن نفس العمل ليس بفسق، اللهم إلا إذا كانوا أعواناً على الظلم، فحينئذ لا تقبل شهادتهم وقيل إن كان العامل وجيها ذا مروءة ولا يجازف في كلامه ولا يحلف عليه كثيرا ولا اعتاد شتم أولاده أو غيرهم تقبل شهادته وإلا لا وقال الفقيه أبو الليث إن كان العامل مثل عمر بن عبد العزيز ونحوه من حيث الديانة تجوز شهادته، وإن كان مثل يزيد بن معاوية ونحوه لا تقبل. والمعتق: أي وكذا تقبل شهادة المعتق - بفتح التاء - للمعتق - بكسر التاء - وكذا بالعكس؛ لعدم التهمة.
And the workers: meaning, thus the testimony of workers (with damm on 'ayn and tashdid on mim, plural of 'amil) is accepted. They are the sultans' workers who collect obligatory dues like kharaj, jizya, and sadaqat according to the majority. They said: This was in their era; in our time their testimony is not accepted due to the prevalence of their oppression. The upshot is that if they are upright, it is accepted; otherwise not. And it is said that by 'workers' is meant the princes; and it is said they are those who work with their hands and hire themselves out. Whichever it is, their testimony is accepted because the work itself is not fisq, unless they are helpers in oppression, then their testimony is not accepted. And it is said if the worker is influential, mannerly, does not speak rashly, does not swear often, and is not accustomed to insulting his children or others, his testimony is accepted; otherwise not. And Abu al-Layth said: if the worker is like 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz and the like in religiosity, his testimony is permitted; if like Yazid ibn Mu'awiya and the like, it is not accepted. And the emancipator: meaning, thus the testimony of the emancipator (with fatha on ta) for the emancipated (with kasra on ta) is accepted, and likewise the opposite, due to lack of suspicion.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on accepting testimony of two categories: 'ummal (workers) and mu'tiq/mu'taq (emancipator/emancipated). For 'ummal, the author first defines them as state tax collectors; early scholars allowed it, but later it became restricted due to common injustice. The core principle is that testimony is based on 'adala (uprightness), not occupation. Various opinions are mentioned: some restrict to workers of upright character, others to those of noble conduct. Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi gives examples of ideal and corrupt rulers. For mu'tiq and mu'taq, testimony is accepted between them and vice versa because there is no suspicion (tuhma) of bias in the relationship; the ruling is clear and unconditional.

Key Terms
العمال (al-'ummal): Workers of the sultan who collect legal dues like kharaj, jizya, and sadaqat; also interpreted as princes or hired laborers.
المعتق (al-mu'tiq): The emancipator (master who frees a slave). Testimony between mu'tiq and mu'taq is accepted due to absence of tuhma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the testimony of an 'ummal (worker) accepted?
A) Always, because work itself is not fisq.
B) Only if he is a prince or ruler.
C) If he is upright ('adil).
D) Never, due to prevalence of oppression.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of testimony in Hanafi fiqh depends on the witness's 'adala, not on occupation or status; the unconditional acceptance between mu'tiq and mu'taq illustrates that bias is not presumed when the relationship is one of benefaction.

Shahadat — Testimony
